  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ab10426d-f6b0-4f73-9fb8-c533f633821b'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<span id="freeTextContainer3009743564094191857">Adventurer Richard Hannay, just returned from South Africa, is thoroughly bored with London life-until he is accosted by a mysterious American, who warns him of an assassination plot that could completely destabalise the fragile political balance of Europe. Initially sceptical, Hannay nonetheless harbours the man-but one dayreturns home to find him murdered... An obvious s</span>
  <span id="freeText3009743564094191857" style="display:none">Adventurer Richard Hannay, just returned from South Africa, is thoroughly bored with London life-until he is accosted by a mysterious American, who warns him of an assassination plot that could completely destabalise the fragile political balance of Europe. Initially sceptical, Hannay nonetheless harbours the man-but one dayreturns home to find him murdered... An obvious suspect, Hannay flees to his native Scotland, pursued by both the police and a cunning, ruthless enemy. His life and the security of Britan are in grave peril, and everything rests on the solution to a baffling enigma: what are the 'thirty nine steps?'<br />First published 1915</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_729061155" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/729061155" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/729061155?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781843175933" href="/book/show/10174388-the-39-steps">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=spies">spies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=book-to-film">book-to-film</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer729061155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12934980143080084816"><b>”I know what it is to feel lonely and helpless and to have the whole world against me, and those are things that no men or women ought to feel.” Richard Hanney in The 39 Steps. </b><br/><br/><a href='http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Richard-Hannay-by-John-Buchan_zps34a64afb.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380564383i/3115679.jpg' alt=' photo Richard-Hannay-by-John-Buchan_zps34a64afb.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>In the edition that I read Toby Buchan, grandson of John Buchan, wrote an introduction that was almost an apology. About half way through the book I understood the need for an apology. The book pales in comparison to the movie. The writing is jaunty and for a while sustains the reader, but soon I was searching despera</span>
  <span id="freeText12934980143080084816" style="display:none"><b>”I know what it is to feel lonely and helpless and to have the whole world against me, and those are things that no men or women ought to feel.” Richard Hanney in The 39 Steps. </b><br><br><a href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Richard-Hannay-by-John-Buchan_zps34a64afb.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380564383i/3115679.jpg" alt=" photo Richard-Hannay-by-John-Buchan_zps34a64afb.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>In the edition that I read Toby Buchan, grandson of John Buchan, wrote an introduction that was almost an apology. About half way through the book I understood the need for an apology. The book pales in comparison to the movie. The writing is jaunty and for a while sustains the reader, but soon I was searching desperately for the dialogue or the scenes that I loved most about the movie. <br><br><b>They are not there.</b> <br><br>Charles Bennett adapted the novel to the screen and Ian Hay wrote the dialogue. They took a Buchan framework and turned it into an entertaining and exciting movie. I recently rewatched <i>The 39 Steps</i> (1935) during one of the Hitchcock weekends on TCM which made me that much more interested in reading the book that inspired the movie. Most of the book is one long chase scene involving motor cars, planes, bicycles, and leg races over hill and dale. There are numerous disguises, car crashes, and one rather large explosion. No overtones of sexual attraction or for that matter... women. It is a boy’s adventure played by a 37 year old man who has made his fortune in Rhodesia and found himself in dire circumstances when he decides to see London. <br><br>Indulge me while I plug the movie. <br><br>I had three favorite scenes from the movie that I hoped would be in the book or at least that there would be other memorable scenes that Bennett and Hay decided not to use. None of these scenes are in the book unfortunately. <br><br>The scene with the farmer’s wife that the writers and Hitchcock managed to convey to the watcher in so brief a span of time how lonely and desperate her life is married to a jealous, older, crusty man with no hope of respite. When the Richard Hanney character played by Robert Donat kisses her as he scrambles out a back door with her husband’s coat and hat I felt like cheering. That kiss, so easy to give, might be the very thing she needs to sustain herself or to break free. <br><br>The scene where Richard Hanney has made it to what he feels is a safe haven only to discover that his benefactor is the very man he has been trying to thwart. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The reveal of the missing digit on the villain's hand is done so well that I still feel the cold, tightness of suspense grip my heart each time. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br><br>In the course of the movie Richard Haney ends up cuffed to a hostile female named Pamela played by Madeleine Carroll. They escape from police custody and end up wet and very annoyed with each other in a room over a bar. They have the police and a pair of henchmen looking for them. In the room she sits down to peel her wet stockings off her legs and because he is cuffed to her his hand travels down each leg with her hands. It is one of the most sensual, sexy scenes in movie history and no one is naked. <br><br><a href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/39-steps_zps072c2484.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380564383i/3115680.jpg" alt=" photo 39-steps_zps072c2484.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Toby Buchan did provide me with a tidbit of information in his introduction to the book that was interesting. The character of Richard Hanney was based off the exploits of Lord Edmund Ironside, 1st Baron Ironside who had a long distinguished British military career. He commanded forces in WWI and WWII ending his career as a Field Marshal. John Buchan when he was writing this book in 1914 was only aware of Edmund “Tiny” Ironside’s exploits during the Boer War.<br><br><b>At the end of the war, he was part of the small force which escorted Jan Smuts to the peace negotiations. He then disguised himself as an Afrikaans-speaking Boer, and took a job as a wagon driver working for the German colonial forces in South West Africa. This intelligence work ended unsuccessfully, however; he was identified, and escaped shortly before being caught. This escapade later led to claims that he was the model for Richard Hannay, a character in the novels of John Buchan; it is interesting to note that Ironside himself enjoyed these novels, reading <i>Mr Standfast</i> in the implausibly romantic setting of the passenger seat of an open-cockpit biplane flying from Iraq to Persia.</b> Wikipedia<br><br><a href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/EdmundIronside_zps27eb613c.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380564383i/3115681.jpg" alt=" photo EdmundIronside_zps27eb613c.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>You can probably guess which one is Edmund “Tiny” Ironside.</b><br><br>I prefer my armchair traveling where I can experience escaping captivity or flying in an open-cockpit biplane from Iraq to Persia from the safety of my oversized leather reading chair, but it does make me feel like my life is...well...a bit pedestrian.<br><br>My advice is to skip the book and go watch the movie.<br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="12934980143080084816" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2033715630" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2033715630" />
    <a title="Bobby Underwood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17994985-bobby-underwood"><img alt="Bobby Underwood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588932697p2/17994985.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2033715630?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bobby Underwood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bobby Underwood" href="/user/show/17994985-bobby-underwood">Bobby Underwood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35449202-the-thirty-nine-steps">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2033715630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7089625282047554879">Scotsman John Buchan’s fabulous The Thirty-Nine Steps is rightly considered a seminal classic in the Adventure/Spy genre and it is for good reason it was on The Guardian’s Best 100 English Novels list at #42.<br/><br/>This exciting tale of espionage defined the man-on-the-run tale in breathless fashion, and was the first of the author’s Richard Hannay tales. What remains remarkable is the contemporary prose. Though it takes place before the first World War, offering insight into the view of what was happe</span>
  <span id="freeText7089625282047554879" style="display:none">Scotsman John Buchan’s fabulous The Thirty-Nine Steps is rightly considered a seminal classic in the Adventure/Spy genre and it is for good reason it was on The Guardian’s Best 100 English Novels list at #42.<br /><br />This exciting tale of espionage defined the man-on-the-run tale in breathless fashion, and was the first of the author’s Richard Hannay tales. What remains remarkable is the contemporary prose. Though it takes place before the first World War, offering insight into the view of what was happening at that time, the tale is timeless, and with minor changes, could easily be a thrilling espionage adventure told in our day. Books need to be judged within their context, and while most do, some don't. This classic has a solid four-star average after hundreds of reviews on Amazon in the US, which accurately reflects how much fun this is to read.<br /><br />That's not to say some of what happens isn't implausible, almost Cornell Woolrich implausible, but with a style and pace which makes Robert Ludlum seem lethargic — no easy task — the reader is having so much fun they simply don’t care. Reading The Thirty-Nine Steps is fun and exciting, which is what it is supposed to be. Watching Hannay escape time after time until the thrilling confrontation and conclusion is exhilarating.<br /><br />Buchan writes as though using lighting bolts rather than a pen, and we’re just along for the electric-charged ride. The Thirty-Nine Steps is the quintessential can’t-put-down read. That thrill you got as a youngster reading a mystery adventure by flashlight beneath the covers was captured by Buchan and moved forward into adulthood, and on that level it doesn't just succeed, it shines. It's on The Guardian's list for good reason.<br /><br />The book differs from Hitchcock’s famous British film adaptation in that there is no love interest for Hannay here; frankly because as a boys adventure story brought into adulthood, it isn’t needed. A rollicking good old-fashioned tale that set a bar seldom reached since. Fabulous fun and quite enjoyable when read, if you don't make comparisons with spy novels written many decades later, and why would you do that? This edition of this seminal work has an excellent biography at the end readers will most likely enjoy. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7089625282047554879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_393397683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/393397683" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/393397683?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141441177" href="/book/show/147114.The_Thirty_Nine_Steps">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Emily May by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer393397683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9263194964150211885">I am currently working my way through the list of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/452208.1001_Books_You_Must_Read_Before_You_Die' title='1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die by Peter Boxall' rel='nofollow noopener'>1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die</a> and decided to read Buchan's short mystery/spy novel because it seemed like a quick and easy option to take me a step closer to maybe one day completing the list. I never imagined it would be such a painfully boring slog. <br/><br/>Some books made the big list because they are actually good, some because they are (or were) scandalous, some because they are so far away from pretty much everything else that's been writ</span>
  <span id="freeText9263194964150211885" style="display:none">I am currently working my way through the list of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/452208.1001_Books_You_Must_Read_Before_You_Die" title="1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die by Peter Boxall" rel="nofollow noopener">1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die</a> and decided to read Buchan's short mystery/spy novel because it seemed like a quick and easy option to take me a step closer to maybe one day completing the list. I never imagined it would be such a painfully boring slog. <br /><br />Some books made the big list because they are actually good, some because they are (or were) scandalous, some because they are so far away from pretty much everything else that's been written, and some because they kick-started something or opened up a new type of genre and/or storytelling. I believe <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/147114.The_Thirty_Nine_Steps" title="The Thirty-Nine Steps by John Buchan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Thirty-Nine Steps</a> falls into this last category. It arguably introduced the world to the "spy" genre and has resulted in many attempted imitations over the years since its publication in 1915. But in terms of plot, writing and characters it just seems to me to have very little to offer. It may be one of the first of its kind, but many other authors have bettered the genre, in my opinion. I would use <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1411964.John_le_Carr_" title="John le Carré" rel="nofollow noopener">John le Carré</a> as a prime example.<br /><br />The novel begins with the bored Richard Hannay who is determined to give London just one more day to hold his interest before he leaves for a more exciting alternative abroad. Richard, however, gets way more than he bargained for when a new American acquaintance is murdered in Hannay's flat just a few days after the pair meet. Realising he is now likely the main target of the group who assassinated his new friend, and realising he will be the police force's main suspect for the murder, Richard takes off on the run around Scotland.<br /><br />Richard is given very little characterization or development, he has no personality and the novel focuses on what happens to him, instead of who he is, why he acts in a particular way, or what he cares about - apart from the desire to avoid capture by the police or the assassins. Though he is being chased by two groups who either want to kill him or lock him up, I got no sense of his fear, desperation or urgency. The novel lacked emotion and I felt like I could be reading a cold, uncaring police report of events, rather than a first-hand account of them. This whole mess seemed like a little inconvenience in Richard Hannay's life, not something that was a real danger to him.<br /><br />Most of all, it was boring. The conclusion wasn't satisfying enough to be worth putting up with the sequence of boring events for. I think <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/262816130" rel="nofollow noopener">this review</a> says a lot about the novel's plot: <i>"He runs around in the fields. A lot. He hides in this field. He hides in that field. Some shadowy figures close in, and off he goes, running again."</i> An excellent and accurate summary, in my opinion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9263194964150211885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1110550579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1110550579" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1110550579?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1110550579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13003162067208221666">Run-of-the-mill outmoded thriller. With conventions that pile on & on like wretched clichés, "The 39 Steps" is somewhat thrilling, somewhat entertaining. A sure predecessor to "The Fugitive," it has our main man running from the law while hiding and acting the parts of the British "lower" classes. The theme being that camouflage is the best defense, while you're out on the offense.<br/><br/>There's reverse psychology, the usurping of identities, and the amateur loss of evidence (here, a motor-car, a bicyc</span>
  <span id="freeText13003162067208221666" style="display:none">Run-of-the-mill outmoded thriller. With conventions that pile on &amp; on like wretched clichés, "The 39 Steps" is somewhat thrilling, somewhat entertaining. A sure predecessor to "The Fugitive," it has our main man running from the law while hiding and acting the parts of the British "lower" classes. The theme being that camouflage is the best defense, while you're out on the offense.<br /><br />There's reverse psychology, the usurping of identities, and the amateur loss of evidence (here, a motor-car, a bicycle). Stupid, gullible people, drunk sometimes, also stumble upon the protagonist just when he needs them, the pre-WWI Good Samaritans, the most. <br /><br />Simply: It's a hide-and-seek in the British countryside. <br /><br />Tame, slightly engrossing, a tad too unspecial in a world filled with more complex and superior stories of detection. Yes, it being a prewar novel, it has some historical value. But still.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13003162067208221666" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1919086079" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1919086079" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1919086079?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1919086079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3958655675030138767"><b>I LIKE THE CUT OF YOUR JIB</b><br/><br/>In this mercifully short ur-thriller our hero is the kind of guy who has an inbuilt trustometer which is activated by looking. He looks at another man and instantly can tell if he’s the decent, upstanding, plucky sort or the low, conniving, blackguard sort. <br/><br/><i>He was very young, but he was the man for my money.</i> P30<br/><br/><i>I saw by this man’s eyes that he was the kind you can trust</i> p43<br/><br/>Other men also have this impressive power of instant worthiness assessment :<br/><br/><i>He watched me with a s</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3958655675030138767" style="display:none"><b>I LIKE THE CUT OF YOUR JIB</b><br /><br />In this mercifully short ur-thriller our hero is the kind of guy who has an inbuilt trustometer which is activated by looking. He looks at another man and instantly can tell if he’s the decent, upstanding, plucky sort or the low, conniving, blackguard sort. <br /><br /><i>He was very young, but he was the man for my money.</i> P30<br /><br /><i>I saw by this man’s eyes that he was the kind you can trust</i> p43<br /><br />Other men also have this impressive power of instant worthiness assessment :<br /><br /><i>He watched me with a smile. “I don’t want proof… I can size up a man. You’re no murderer and you’re no fool. I believe you are speaking the truth.” </i><br /><br />Early on, our man Richard Hannay runs into an odd cove called Scudder and they use their trustometers on each other :<br /><br /><i> “Just one word, Mr Scudder. I believe you are straight, but if so be you are not, I should warn you that I’m a handy man with a gun.” </i><br /><br />Scudder says <br /><br /><i> “I haven’t the privilege of your name, sir, but let me tell you that you’re a white man. I’ll thank you to lend me a razor.” </i><br /><br />I was thinking well, you don’t have to be too perspicacious to see that someone is white and not black, but then I realized that white in this context does not mean white. Of course, it means “good”. <br /><br />So this is not <i>The Wire</i>. It’s more like the 1914 version of James Bond, meaning no technology, absolutely no girls, but lots of racing around. Even though it’s 1914 cars are written off. This whole novel is one long chase scene.<br /><br /><b>EXIT PURSUED BY A JEW</b><br /><br />Like certain popular songs where the verses are something you have to endure in order to get to the great singalong chorus John Buchan has to provide us with some kind of explanation for all this lying low, adopting disguises, cracking cyphers and running around. So Scudder explains that there is a dastardly German organization operating in England called the Black Stone. They are trying to steal military secrets and assassinate foreign politicians! I think anyway, it’s not awfully clear. And why? Let Scudder explain:<br /><br /><i>Away behind all the governments and the armies there was a big subterranean movement going on, engineered by very dangerous people… I gathered that most of the people in it were sort of educated anarchists that make revolutions, but that beside them were financiers who were playing for money. A clever man can make big profits on a falling market, and it suited the book of both classes to set Europe by the ears. … When I asked why, he said that the anarchist lot thought it would give them their chance. Everything would be in the melting pot, and they looked to see a new world emerge. The capitalists would rake in the shekels, and make fortuned by buying up the wreckage. Capital, he said, had no conscience and no fatherland. Besides, the Jew was behind it.</i><br /><br />A nice summary of what certain people must have been thinking as Europe did indeed slide into war and the Russian revolution was just around the corner. I bet Adolf was a fan of <i>The Thirty-Nine Steps</i>.<br /><br />So for the first half the Black Stone is pursuing Richard Hannay, our well-heeled ex-colonial, and for the second half he (and the British government) is pursuing them. This novel is somewhat past its sell-by date. I think its sell-by date was June 1915.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3958655675030138767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8009916130300317849" style="display:none">Read as part of <a href="https://infiniteanddarling.co.uk/blogs/infinite-variety-books/the-infinite-variety-reading-challenge-part-one" rel="nofollow noopener">The Infinite Variety Reading Challenge</a>, based on the BBC's Big Read Poll of 2003.<br /><br />A fairly conceited man gets embroiled in a rather far-fetched murder-cum-political-conspiracy that can only be described as Man Walks Through A Lot Of Heather. Mercifully short, this book could have been even shorter if we didn't have to follow Mr Hannay the length and breadth of Scotland, only to hear about his aching feet.<br /><br />Fairly regular stuff, adventurous without too much danger to quicken your pace-maker. The only thing that was really missing was a James Bond-style Woman-For-Looking-At-And-Not-Much-Else-(Oh-Yes-Sleeping-With-Too). Not an awful lot of depth even if it was purposefully written that way, though that's hardly an excuse. It was also lacking in any kind of depth in terms of plot (there's a conspiracy, but what it is no-one really knows an awful lot about it: handy).<br /><br />Short, not that sweet, but a vaguely interesting run-of-the-mill wee read for if you miss the train and don't have Bradshaw to hand. <div id="review_1008654897" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1008654897" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1008654897?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1008654897" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16549517778927969283">I hadn't heard of this book until recently, when it made a surprise appearance on The Guardian's <a href='http://www.theguardian.com/books/series/the-100-best-novels' title='Link to their list' rel='nofollow noopener'>Best 100 English Novels</a> list. It's an early spy novel, written in 1915 and set just before WWI, and a smashing and brisk read. It was written by a John Buchan, 1st Baron Tweedsmuir, and I did not make that up. Baron Tweedsmuir. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406638958i/10576904.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>Baron Tweedsmuir, at your service sirrah</i><br/><br/>It cites <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/246047435' title='My review of Kipling's spy novel Kim' rel='nofollow noopener'>Kipling</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217035607' title='My review of Conrad's spy novel The Secret Agent' rel='nofollow noopener'>Conrad</a> as influences, appropriately, and there's some mention of Holmes as well, but its primary influence is clea</span>
  <span id="freeText16549517778927969283" style="display:none">I hadn't heard of this book until recently, when it made a surprise appearance on The Guardian's <a href="http://www.theguardian.com/books/series/the-100-best-novels" title="Link to their list" rel="nofollow noopener">Best 100 English Novels</a> list. It's an early spy novel, written in 1915 and set just before WWI, and a smashing and brisk read. It was written by a John Buchan, 1st Baron Tweedsmuir, and I did not make that up. Baron Tweedsmuir. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406638958i/10576904.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Baron Tweedsmuir, at your service sirrah</i><br><br>It cites <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/246047435" title="My review of Kipling's spy novel Kim" rel="nofollow noopener">Kipling</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217035607" title="My review of Conrad's spy novel The Secret Agent" rel="nofollow noopener">Conrad</a> as influences, appropriately, and there's some mention of Holmes as well, but its primary influence is clearly Robert Louis Stevenson's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1002471550" title="My review of Kidnapped" rel="nofollow noopener">Kidnapped.</a> There's a scene involving hiding and sweltering on top of a dovecote that's a direct play on a similar one in Kidnapped, but above all they share Scottishness, which manifests itself in a love of running about on moors and in a general unawareness of the existence of women. There are zero women in this book. Seriously, you never even pass one on the sidewalk. (Correction: a commenter named Vesna says there is one. I don't remember her but I'm willing to believe it.)<br><br><b>Top Six Literary Works Featuring Moors</b><br><br>6. The 39 Steps<br>5. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154862670" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Hound of the Baskervilles</a><br>4. Kidnapped<br>3. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2264738740" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Return of the Native</a><br>2. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116260888" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Othello</a><br>1. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154863435" title="My review of Wuthering Heights, a book I dearly love" rel="nofollow noopener">Wuthering Heights</a><br><br>What Buchan is really, really into is disguises:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406638647i/10576891.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>like this but no parrot</i><br><br>And very little happens in The 39 Steps that doesn't have to do with them. Buchan's hero, Richard Hannay, is a master of disguise: his transformation into a road worker at one point is wonderfully detailed. His Moriartyesque nemesis is even better, which leads to a denouement that isn't really believable but gets the job done.<br><br>This is more of a novella than a novel, and - arguably aside from some semi-interesting talk about the philosophy of disguising oneself - it's not very deep. It's a nonstop thrill ride, is what it is. But it's a hell of a good time. <br><br>You know what else is a good time is just saying Baron Tweedsmuir. Hello, Baron Tweedsmuir. We meet again, Baron Tweedsmuir.<br><br>Tweedsmuir.</span>

<div id="review_72656940" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72656940" />
    <a title="Michael P." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2269105-michael-p"><img alt="Michael P." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1278431856p2/2269105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72656940?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael P." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael P." href="/user/show/2269105-michael-p">Michael P.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330106962" href="/book/show/2155044.The_Thirty_Nine_Steps">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72656940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16900188334752919633">How can a classic be so bad? Melodramatic, as expected, but Buchan piles improbability upon improbability insulting your intelligence until by the end you just want to slap him. This is an important book in that it sprung many imitators, and some claim it is the start of the spy genre. It has been filmed three times, adapted for radio and television, inspired the chase film genre, and certainly it gave Alfred Hitchcock his basic subject. Buchan was a political man, and he uses the book for a lit</span>
  <span id="freeText16900188334752919633" style="display:none">How can a classic be so bad? Melodramatic, as expected, but Buchan piles improbability upon improbability insulting your intelligence until by the end you just want to slap him. This is an important book in that it sprung many imitators, and some claim it is the start of the spy genre. It has been filmed three times, adapted for radio and television, inspired the chase film genre, and certainly it gave Alfred Hitchcock his basic subject. Buchan was a political man, and he uses the book for a little bit of political and social satire. Well and good, but the ridiculous plot, narrative short cuts, and silly (but always convincing to the other characters) disguises make this a bad, bad book. It has one of the least credible and least exciting endings I have read in a thriller: no wonder all the films change it. Yet, credit due, Buchan invented a lot narratively that became part of popular culture, and has found a compelling voice for his first person narrator. The book is every bit as readable as it is bad, so readable that I’ll probably look for one of his later books to see if Buchan learned how to plot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16900188334752919633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1980692849" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1980692849" />
    <a title="Whispering Stories" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20354973-whispering-stories"><img alt="Whispering Stories" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548065529p2/20354973.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1980692849?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whispering Stories" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whispering Stories" href="/user/show/20354973-whispering-stories">Whispering Stories</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847496454" href="/book/show/32710780-the-39-steps">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1980692849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5419912810301261911">When it was first published, this novel must have been fascinating reading. At the time the UK was at war with Germany and there were no doubt German spies in the country. The book was initially serialised in a magazine and many chapters end on the proverbial cliff hanger. As a result the story is fast paced and full of action.<br/><br/>In a dedication before the book John Buchan describes the book as a “dime novel” or “shocker” where ‘… the incidents defy the probabilities and march just inside the borde</span>
  <span id="freeText5419912810301261911" style="display:none">When it was first published, this novel must have been fascinating reading. At the time the UK was at war with Germany and there were no doubt German spies in the country. The book was initially serialised in a magazine and many chapters end on the proverbial cliff hanger. As a result the story is fast paced and full of action.<br /><br />In a dedication before the book John Buchan describes the book as a “dime novel” or “shocker” where ‘… the incidents defy the probabilities and march just inside the borders of the possible’. I cannot put it better than that.<br /><br />The lead character of Richard Hannay is a wealthy man in his late ‘thirties who has recently returned from successful business activities in Africa. Bored with London society he initially relishes the intrigue offered by his chance meeting with Scudder but his situation soon deteriorates.<br /><br />I found the Hannay and the other leading characters somewhat stereotypical but that is not altogether surprising in an action novel of this length. I suspect Buchan’s target audience did not want depth and sensitivity; they wanted easy to understand characters and lots of action.<br /><br />I particularly enjoyed the descriptions of country life in Galloway which would then have been a world away from life in an English city.<br />Yes, it may seem a bit thin and dated but before you question its definition as a Classic novel, consider the thousands of spy thrillers published in the intervening century which follow the same format. I am sure we have all read work from authors who could well have been influenced by John Buchan.<br /><br />The Thirty Nine Steps deserves a read if only for its historical status. I have awarded it three stars.<br /><br />Reviewed by Clive on <a href="http://www.whisperingstories.com" rel="nofollow noopener">www.whisperingstories.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5419912810301261911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_948314173" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/948314173" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4542979-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454402751p2/4542979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/948314173?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4542979-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18680050-the-thirty-nine-steps">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer948314173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9361206991347745663"><br/>This is a novel the literary importance of which I have no trouble appreciating. First published in 1915, it's the ancestor of the espionage thriller genre featuring the rugged-man-of-action-on-the-run style of hero. I would probably have enjoyed it more if I was a regular reader of that genre. I'm not and consequently I was distinctly underwhelmed. <br/><br/>What I didn't like about the work first. For me, the main problem is that the plot pushes the concept of implausibility to its extreme limits. I'm g</span>
  <span id="freeText9361206991347745663" style="display:none"><br />This is a novel the literary importance of which I have no trouble appreciating. First published in 1915, it's the ancestor of the espionage thriller genre featuring the rugged-man-of-action-on-the-run style of hero. I would probably have enjoyed it more if I was a regular reader of that genre. I'm not and consequently I was distinctly underwhelmed. <br /><br />What I didn't like about the work first. For me, the main problem is that the plot pushes the concept of implausibility to its extreme limits. I'm generally quite willing to suspend disbelief - an attitude instilled in me by years of reading crime fiction - but I had a lot difficulty doing so in this instance. The hero, Richard Hannay, who is back in London after a long spell doing this and that in South Africa, gets caught up in a conspiracy relating to a German spy ring. Simultaneously on the run from the police who believe him implicated in a murder and the said German spy ring, Hannay spends a lot of time in disguise - luckily other people's clothes seem to fit him perfectly - and the rest of his time legging it across the Scottish and English landscape. His escapes are frankly ludicrous and the final scene defies any degree of willingness to accept the implausible and go along for the ride. <br /><br />Another problem is the complete lack of character development. Hannay is a first person narrator who has the potential to be interesting, but he displays little personality and no psychological depth. He's pretty much cardboard-cut-out-man from beginning to end. The villains are suitably evil, but they have no more impact on the reader than the rest of the characters. <br /><br />There are some positives, though. The prose is good and the pacing is in keeping with the action. And the fact that this is the novel which generated so many fictional and film heroes cannot be disregarded. Indeed, the cultural significance of the work is such that before picking it up I was convinced that I must have read it before. How could I not have done so, when it's so well-known? But I'm pretty sure now that it was a first time read, although I've possibly seen the Hitchcock film adaptation. <br /><br />In terms of my reaction to novel as a piece of writing it gets two stars. Another one is thrown in because of its iconic status.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9361206991347745663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/446816120?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio Cassette 9781858480565" href="/book/show/2599505-the-thirty-nine-steps">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer446816120" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10117432525076963126">Richard Hannay came to Great Britain for a break; by the end of his stay he was completely bored. So when a seemingly crazy man asks him for help saying he knows too much and his life is in danger Richard invites the stranger to his apartment. Predictably the host soon finds his guest dead, and now Richard Hannay is on the run from desperate people who stop at nothing to silence him as well. This description sounds a little melodramatic, but so it the book itself.<br/><br/>The book was very influential at</span>
  <span id="freeText10117432525076963126" style="display:none">Richard Hannay came to Great Britain for a break; by the end of his stay he was completely bored. So when a seemingly crazy man asks him for help saying he knows too much and his life is in danger Richard invites the stranger to his apartment. Predictably the host soon finds his guest dead, and now Richard Hannay is on the run from desperate people who stop at nothing to silence him as well. This description sounds a little melodramatic, but so it the book itself.<br /><br />The book was very influential at the time of its publication; some people credit it with the creation of spy thriller genre. Still, it shows its age somewhat. It also has some situations I found hard to believe. For example, why did not the bad guys kill Richard Hannay in the beginning together with his guest thus insuring they remove all people with dangerous knowledge? By the way, this question applies to 90% of all thrillers. <br /><br />Richard Hannay proved to be very ingenuous in trying to stay undercover, I give him this. Even with the problems I mentioned the book is not boring with 3 solid star rating. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10117432525076963126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText14411361237202618296" style="display:none">"The Thirty-Nine Steps" is a classic "innocent man on the run" adventure. Richard Hanney is housing his neighbor who is being followed by an anarchist gang called Black Stone. It's 1914 and the German gang is trying to steal British military plans. When Hanney returns to his flat, he finds the neighbor with a knife in his chest.<br /><br />Hanney feels that he will be arrested for the murder so he takes off to Scotland. He's being hunted down by both the police and the Black Stone gang. Hanney can talk his way out of bad situations, and is a master of disguising himself as part of the working class. The short book is an action-packed adventure that keeps one turning the pages. The story is also full of lucky coincidences and improbable situations. It's a thriller with a patriotic, energetic hero, evil villains, and lots of Scottish local color.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12600071349331600897">The Thirty-Nine Steps (1915) was published just prior to the start of WWI, and the political intrigue surrounding that figured prominently in the plot. So it's an international spy mystery with just a hint of comic relief. I listened to the LibreVox recording, narrated by Adrian Praetzellis, and was thoroughly entertained. His gift of imitating accents and applying his voice so well to the different characters set a tone that I would have missed if I had read the book. </span>

  <span id="freeText14682728811389502133" style="display:none">So bored on a visit to 1915 London that he's on the verge of returning to Rhodesia, mining magnate Richard Hannay is suddenly pulled into adventure of the highest order with Britain's national security at stake.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_378677990" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/378677990" />
    <a title="Dfordoom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1048045-dfordoom"><img alt="Dfordoom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207231748p2/1048045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/378677990?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dfordoom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dfordoom" href="/user/show/1048045-dfordoom">Dfordoom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1048045-dfordoom?shelf=spy-fiction">spy-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer378677990" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18410214811668458465">The Thirty-Nine Steps, published in 1915, was the first of Scottish novelist John Buchan’s five Richard Hannay espionage novels.<br/><br/>Buchan produced both fiction and non-fiction and wrote in a variety of genres including some excellent horror stories and even what could be described as a paranormal adventure novel (The Gap in the Curtain). Buchan was also a successful politician and ended his career as governor-General of Canada (as Lord Tweedsmuir).<br/><br/>But it is for the Richard Hannay novels that he is </span>
  <span id="freeText18410214811668458465" style="display:none">The Thirty-Nine Steps, published in 1915, was the first of Scottish novelist John Buchan’s five Richard Hannay espionage novels.<br /><br />Buchan produced both fiction and non-fiction and wrote in a variety of genres including some excellent horror stories and even what could be described as a paranormal adventure novel (The Gap in the Curtain). Buchan was also a successful politician and ended his career as governor-General of Canada (as Lord Tweedsmuir).<br /><br />But it is for the Richard Hannay novels that he is now best remembered. <br /><br />The Thirty-Nine Steps is a classic chase story. South African Richard Hannay is bored by life in London but he is about to get more adventure than he’s bargained for when he meets the mysterious Scudder. Scudder is an intelligence agent and his story about the assassination of a Balkan political leader seems fantastic to Hannay. Then Scudder is killed by enemy agents and Hannay finds himself in possession of a secret that could cost him his life.<br /><br />Being uncomfortable with city life at the best of times he decides he would have a better chance of survival in some wild place where his experiences on the veldt would stand him in good stead. So he heads off in the direction of the Scottish Highlands. He is on the run not only from the German spy network but also from the police, being a suspect in the murder of Scudder.<br /><br />While evading capture Hannay also has to puzzle out the full meaning of the conspiracy uncovered by Scudder, and it goes far beyond anything he originally imagined. It is nothing less than a plan for destroying the British fleet on the outbreak of war. But what is the meaning of the thirty-nine steps that keep getting mentioned in Scudder’s notebooks? And how can Hannay, a mere civilian, convince the British government of the truth of this amazing story? He will need evidence. So while Hannay is the hunted he is about to turn hunter.<br /><br />Hitchcock’s classic 1935 film version is the best-known of the several movie adaptations of this novel. The plot of the 1935 movie differs quite markedly from the plot of the book. In the book the thirty-nine steps is not a mere McGuffin as in the film (a McGuffin being something of no importance in itself except insofar as both the heroes and the villains happen to be seeking it). In the book the thirty-nine steps are crucially important and the espionage conspiracy takes centre stage (while the movie is essentially just a chase movie, albeit one of the greatest such movies ever made).<br /><br />Richard Hannay is one of the great fictional spy heroes, a rather taciturn but very determined character who is driven by both patriotism and a thirst for adventure. <br /><br />Buchan’s novel is a classic of the spy genre and is a must-read for any spy fan.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18410214811668458465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span id="freeText17814673971080126389" style="display:none">Thanks to the extremely cheap "Penguin Classics" series, this summer I've had a chance to catch up on a heap of books I might not otherwise have read. In the spy-thriller genre, there was Erskine Childers' "Riddle of the Sands", and this book by John Buchan.<br /><br />Of course, I'd seen the Hitchcock film, but didn't really remember much of it. Someone posted a question, wondering if the book matches the excellence of the movie. In a word: "absolutely". I read the first chapter several weeks ago, then put the book aside. When I picked it up again last night, I finished it in one sitting (it is fewer than 100 pages).<br /><br />Nothing profound, or life-changing - just an excellent story, excitingly told. I highly recommend this book.</span>

<div id="review_207472786" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207472786" />
    <a title="Bill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4380068-bill"><img alt="Bill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600374592p2/4380068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207472786?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill" href="/user/show/4380068-bill">Bill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330106962" href="/book/show/2155044.The_Thirty_Nine_Steps">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=spy">spy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4380068-bill?shelf=2019-indiv-challenges">2019-indiv-challenges</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207472786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7845906180229708169">This is a classic, an interesting, exciting story. It's similar but at the same time a fair bit different from Alfred Hitchcock's movie. Interesting story of a man caught up in extraordinary events which have the potential to dramatically change the outcome of the impending war, who uses his ingenuity and basic smarts to avoid his enemies. I'll leave it there as I don't want to spoil for anyone who hasn't read before. Excellent story.<br/><br/>Reread - I enjoyed this reread even more than the other times </span>
  <span id="freeText7845906180229708169" style="display:none">This is a classic, an interesting, exciting story. It's similar but at the same time a fair bit different from Alfred Hitchcock's movie. Interesting story of a man caught up in extraordinary events which have the potential to dramatically change the outcome of the impending war, who uses his ingenuity and basic smarts to avoid his enemies. I'll leave it there as I don't want to spoil for anyone who hasn't read before. Excellent story.<br /><br />Reread - I enjoyed this reread even more than the other times I've read. As it was for a book group I took a bit more time. Hitchcock's movie was basically quite similar except for the addition of the young lady. It's a fast-paced adventure and Buchan wastes no time getting to the crux.<br /><br />Hannay, bored with London, has decided to return to South Africa where he has worked as an engineer. He decides on a night out on the town and when he returns home, his upstairs neighbour, Scudder asks for his help. Scudder is aware of a plot to destroy the British fleet but fears he is being followed and he hides out in Hannay's flat. After a few days, Hannay finds Scudder stabbed to death and this begins Hannay's journey to Scotland to avoid the Black Stone and bide his time until he can advise the British government of the plot. To do so precipitously will cause the enemy to change their plans.<br /><br />The black book of Scudder is in code but Hannay figures out the gist of it, including a reference to 39 Steps. Hannay must evade both the police (as he is a suspect for Scudder's murder) and also the Black Stone (German spies) as he races through the Scottish highlands. He meets many interesting helpful people who help him on his way. Eventually Hannay will get with the government and there will be another race to try and capture the enemy. <br /><br />Action-packed story. Hannay is a common-sense individual who relies on his instincts, a strong, enjoyable character and an exciting story. Hannay appeared in at least 4 other Buchan adventures. Worth reading them all. (4 stars)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7845906180229708169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_262816130" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/262816130" />
    <a title="Kristopher Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2784636-kristopher-kelly"><img alt="Kristopher Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442606595p2/2784636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/262816130?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristopher Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristopher Kelly" href="/user/show/2784636-kristopher-kelly">Kristopher Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer262816130&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer262816130" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12562987384863303314">Richard Hannay's been feeling bored with his life in London. Reading the paper one morning, Hannay sees something about a politician he admires, and next thing he knows, he's conjured an anti-semite out of thin air to spin yarns in his parlor and tell him there is a plot to kill the admirable politician and launch Britain and Germany into war. Luckily for Hannay, this anti-semite is murdered mysteriously, leaving Hannay looking pretty suspicious, so what can he do but become the author's wish-fu</span>
  <span id="freeText12562987384863303314" style="display:none">Richard Hannay's been feeling bored with his life in London. Reading the paper one morning, Hannay sees something about a politician he admires, and next thing he knows, he's conjured an anti-semite out of thin air to spin yarns in his parlor and tell him there is a plot to kill the admirable politician and launch Britain and Germany into war. Luckily for Hannay, this anti-semite is murdered mysteriously, leaving Hannay looking pretty suspicious, so what can he do but become the author's wish-fulfillment and go on the run and engage in a little international espionage. <br /><br />By which I mean he runs around in the fields. A lot. He hides in this field. He hides in that field. Some shadowy figures close in, and off he goes, running again. <br /><br />I much prefer the move version, Sweet Sweetback's Baadasssss Song. At least that has good music for all the running around parts. <br /><br />This book is a series of improbable scenes of a man adopting various disguises to avoid detection while he does next to nothing of any import -- until the final chapter, where he unravels it all in one of the most ridiculous scenes I have ever read. Seriously. He realizes that the man sitting right in front of him with NO DISGUISE ON is a man he met and had a conversation with a few chapters earlier. And it's treated like an ah-ha! moment. <br /><br />Credit where it's due, I suppose for being one of the first of its kind. Rumor has it this book started the spy genre. If so, I wish they'd had a better blueprint. This is one of the worst books I've ever read. It has little resemblance to the Hitchcock film of the same title. <br /><br />And they call it a classic ... </span>
  <a data-text-id="12562987384863303314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
